Morning Brew · Finimize · Tech Brew ▾
The first device from the maker of ChatGPT will be an Alexa competitor designed to seem alive, Bloomberg reported. The same week, China issued the exact opposite instruction — disable the features that make users emotionally dependent on chatbots.
  • The device: due next year, a screenless smart speaker carrying ChatGPT's latest, most conversational Voice Mode, plus moving "mechanical elements" meant to create "a sense that it is alive." A camera and sensors help it understand the user's surroundings and context; a rechargeable battery invites you to carry it room to room. It becomes more personalised and proactive over time by reading your digital information, like emails
  • What could derail it: last week Apple sued OpenAI for stealing trade secrets through some of the 400+ former Apple employees who defected — and OpenAI's hardware effort is staffed with ex-Apple designers and engineers. OpenAI denies it. Also on the roadmap: home robotics, an AI necklace, and a smartphone-like device — but the lawsuit could reset the timeline
  • China's opposite order: new rules took effect requiring tech firms to disable features that draw users into emotionally dependent relationships with AI, in the hope of pushing a dwindling population to have children again. China's population fell for a fourth straight year in 2025 and its birthrate hit its lowest level since 1949. ByteDance, Alibaba and Tencent's chatbots can no longer tell users everything they say is cute; minors are banned from bot relationships and pre-release oversight tightens
  • Why the urgency — Tencent Research Institute's April survey of 18-to-40-year-olds: over 70% had developed a dependence on AI, nearly 80% had felt "AI understands me", and on difficult subjects 56% turned to AI while only 14% confided in a fellow human
💡 Big-picture link

On the same technology, a US company is betting money on "more alive" while a government is legislating "less alive." Since the Apr 30 OpenAI shock the axis of competition has been model capability; it is now shifting to devices (distribution) and regulation (social tolerance). And the Apple suit extends Jul 13: Apple didn't spend on AI — it litigates instead.

AI Liability
(7/16) xAI sues its own user — the first time an AI lab has sued a customer, and the "neutral tool" defence
Tech Brew · WSJ ▾
xAI has sued a man for allegedly using Grok to make child sexual abuse material — likely the first time an AI lab has sued one of its own users over how they used its product. The irony: xAI is doing this while facing a wave of lawsuits blaming its model for generating nonconsensual sexual imagery.
  • A very Musk opening: the 12-page complaint begins with a screenshot of his own January 2026 X post — "Anyone using Grok to make illegal content will suffer the same consequences as if they upload illegal content."
  • The defendant is 67-year-old Terry Harwood, accused of "repeated, deliberate, and unconscionable violations" of the terms by bypassing safeguards. The load-bearing claim: Grok is a "neutral tool, subject to user control," and Harwood "designed misleading prompts" to make it comply
  • ⚠️ The awkward part: this argument comes from the company that shipped "Spicy Mode" for explicit image and video generation last August, flooding X with nonconsensual deepfakes. Per a Center for Countering Digital Hate analysis, Grok created roughly 3 million sexualised images — including of children — over just 11 days from December 2025 to January
  • The precedent: the closest case is Microsoft's 2024 suit against a cybercrime ring that stole API keys and hacked the DALL-E generator offered through Azure. But there's no evidence Harwood hacked Grok — meaning xAI is assigning liability to a user of built-in features
  • xAI is fighting deepfake lawsuits in multiple countries; Harwood already faces criminal charges in South Carolina. xAI wants him to cover its own financial losses if his alleged victims come after the company too
  • The social temperature: per the WSJ, violent threats against AI companies are rising and have executives "fearing for their lives." On the other side, George Lucas says AI sceptics are like people clinging to "the horse and the buggy"
💡 Big-picture link

This week's AI axis isn't capability — it's liability and social licence: China outlawed emotional-dependency features, xAI is trying to push responsibility onto users, and executives are worrying about their safety. It matters because of the balance sheet: if harm caused by a model is the user's fault, AI capex is a safe investment; if it's the company's, it's a liability nobody has booked yet. Same bedrock as Dimon's "anti-rich" resentment on Jul 15.

Apple / Anthropic
(7/16) The last piece of the Apple story — a month with Siri AI returns a verdict of "Signal," and Anthropic edges closer to IPO
Tech Brew · Morning Brew ▾
Tech Brew ran the new agentic Siri daily since the developer beta landed on June 11 and delivered its verdict: Signal. "Worth the wait — mostly." The same day brought a report that Anthropic is one step closer to its IPO.
  • The good: the on-device experience, not the app, is the star — a Dynamic Island interface, a slick speaking waveform, and visual cards instead of walls of text. The best use of the agentic features is search: asked for a friend's address texted months earlier and never saved, Siri dug it out of the messages. Same for finding particular photos
  • The complicated: its agentic reach stops at Apple's own apps for now. It couldn't buy Broadway tickets in TodayTix ("I can't search within the TodayTix app right now"), and asked to erase strangers from a photo it merely opened the Clean Up tool. The bad: occasionally the loading swirl vanished with no answer and no record of the question
  • The verdict that matters: "It won't unseat ChatGPT, Claude, or Gemini as your do-everything chatbot, but those can't rummage through your texts and camera roll as seamlessly. For the things that live on your phone, this is the most useful Siri has ever been." — Apple's battlefield is not the model, it's data access
  • Timing: beta signups opened Monday on the iOS 27 public beta; official release with iOS 27 in September, free, but only on iPhone 15 Pro or newer
  • Also: Anthropic is reportedly one step closer to its IPO (extending the Goldman/Morgan Stanley underwriting line from Jul 14) · AppleCare+ is getting a price hike for Macs and iPads too — inflation arriving as service fees rather than device prices · the NTSB's preliminary report says the driver overrode the self-driving software in last month's fatal Texas Tesla crash
💡 Big-picture link

Jul 13 "the money it didn't spend is the earnings" → Jul 15 Alibaba's Qwen solves China, stock hits a record → today confirmation the product actually works. Apple's hat-trick is complete — and how it won is the point: not by building the best model, but by bolting a model onto data it already had, while protecting $140B of free cash flow. Anthropic's IPO is the opposite route: hand the capex bill to the public market.

EV / Credit
(7/15) Lucid: −40% intraday, then +28.79% — an experiment in how fast markets will believe the worst
Brew Markets ▾
Shares of EV maker Lucid plunged as much as 40% intraday the previous day before closing down 16%, after reports that the company was weighing either going private or filing for Chapter 11. Then the stock rebounded 28.79% after Lucid called those reports "completely false."
  • Why the market believed it — the industry: EVs have been under pressure ever since the Trump administration eliminated the $7,500 federal tax credit for new EV purchases
  • Why the market believed it — the company: Lucid isn't expected to be cash-flow positive until 2029, recently missed Wall Street's delivery expectations, and announced plans to lay off 18% of its workforce
  • The rebuttal: "The company has sufficient liquidity to carry its operations well into next year, as recently published in its last quarterly filings, and it has not formed any special Board committee to explore the scenarios reported."
  • The balance sheet backs it: Wall Street expects Lucid to burn about $2.8 billion over the next 12 months, while it ended March with roughly $3.2 billion of liquidity and raised another $1 billion in April. It will, eventually, need to raise more to reach profitability
  • What remains: the reports were false, but they showed how quickly the market is willing to believe the worst. Next month's earnings need to give investors something more tangible to trade on
💡 Big-picture link

In one week, PayPal +17% on a rumour and Lucid −40% then +29%. The index is quiet while single-stock amplitude is extreme — which is exactly why Jul 14's bank trading revenues were the best on record. What this market is selling isn't direction, it's volatility, and every day supplies a fresh exhibit.

July 13–15, 2026
Mon–Wed — a 20% toll reversed in 24 hours, CPI's first fall, record bank profits
Jul 13
Hormuz / Oil
(7/13–14) Hormuz closed again → Trump's 20% toll → reversed within 24 hours — oil's biggest daily gain since 2020
Bloomberg · John Authers / Morning Brew · Brew Markets ▾
The US and Iran traded missiles over the weekend. The US hit 140 military targets on Saturday and dozens more on Sunday; Iran struck US allies across Qatar, Kuwait, Jordan, Oman and Bahrain. The trigger: Iran attacked a Cyprus-flagged container ship in the Strait. Tehran then said it had fully closed Hormuz again, and Brent rallied 5% at Monday's open to $79 (+12.7% off this month's low). By Tuesday crude topped $80 in its largest single-day gain since 2020.
  • Jul 13, Trump's gambit: he reimposed the naval blockade, declared the US the Strait's "guardian," and said it would charge 20% of cargo value to escort ships through. Bloomberg estimated that at roughly $30 million for a loaded supertanker — several times the ~$2 million Iran had been charging — against a norm of 2–3% of cargo value. The UN's IMO opposed it as contrary to international law
  • Jul 14, reversed in a day — replaced, Trump said, by "MASSIVE" Gulf investment in the US. The blockade of Iranian ports resumed anyway
  • Authers: June's Memorandum of Understanding was always ambiguous about exactly who controls the Strait and on what terms — and is now a dead letter. December Brent is more expensive than when the MOU was signed
  • The structural weak point: global oil inventories (public and private) sit at depths unseen since 1984 — making a long blockade progressively harder to sustain
  • BCA's Marko Papic: "oil prices are an independent variable, not a dependent one" — when prices are low, military activity ramps up; when they rise, negotiations return. So June's over-rapid fall in oil is what let the conflict re-escalate. And markets no longer treat the Strait as binary but as a continuum (the 1980s Tanker Wars model) — which limits the reaction
  • The spillover: fertilizer is the war's biggest casualty — Hormuz is a vital conduit for urea, and prices rose over 30% early in the conflict. Add El Niño crop risk and food inflation could turn sticky — the kind of inflation central banks can do nothing about
💡 Big-picture link

Authers' Memorandum of Misunderstanding from Jul 9 is now literally void. The deeper point is Papic's: markets create the facts. Cheap oil emboldens the fighting; expensive oil forces talks. This war cannot settle itself.

CPI / Warsh
(7/14) CPI fell for the first time since 2020 — and Warsh still refused to say "mission accomplished"
Bloomberg · John Authers / Brew Markets · Morning Brew ▾
June consumer prices fell 0.4% on the month — the first monthly decline since 2020, helped by the biggest drop in gasoline since 2022. Headline inflation eased to 3.5%, and core was flat on the month at 2.6% annually. A day earlier, Governor Waller had warned that "if we get another hot reading on core inflation this week, the FOMC will need to consider tightening" — pushing July hike odds to a coin flip. The reversal was dramatic.
  • The breadth: all four main components fell year-on-year. The Fed's supercore (services ex-housing) declined (though still above 3%), the Atlanta Fed's sticky-price gauge hit its lowest since the pandemic, and the Cleveland Fed's trimmed mean dipped below 3%
  • Oxford Economics: of the Fed's three inflation worries, tariff effects weren't discernible and AI-related price pressure wasn't as evident as expected; some oil pass-through showed up and may take longer to work through
  • The market: odds of a hold rose 58%→83% and hike odds fell 42%→16%. Per Deutsche Bank it was the second-largest repricing for the next meeting after a CPI release since 2008. One-year inflation swaps dropped below 2% for the first time since the 2024 election
  • Warsh's maiden congressional testimony: "There might be some who look at today's data and say 'mission accomplished.' That is not my view." · "I'm not for cherry picking" · and no commitment to hikes either. His framing: getting policy right is "the star we steer by"
  • ⚠️ But real yields are the highest since 2023, having surpassed even last year's Liberation Day peak. And with oil back up, July won't be another negative print — Capital Economics: a hike is a matter of when, not if
💡 Big-picture link

Authers called it "Reversal Day" — the toll and the hike scare both flipped within 24 hours. The direction, though, hasn't changed: inflation is still too high to cut, and the next move is still up. This is exactly why Warsh cut his statement to 130 words in June — you can't be forced to reverse a promise you never made.

Bank Earnings
(7/14) Five banks earned $49B in a quarter — JPMorgan's biggest profit in banking history, Goldman's best trading haul ever
Brew Markets · Morning Brew · Finimize · Bloomberg ▾
On "Super Tuesday," America's five biggest banks reported together — and the numbers were overwhelming: $49 billion in combined profit. The irony sits at the center: the money came from the volatility created by the Iran war and the AI panic.
  • JPMorgan: profit +41% to $21.2B — the highest in banking history (including a $4.6B one-off from selling its Visa stake). Goldman Sachs: +78% to $6.6B, with equities trading +72% to $7.4B, a Wall Street record. Bank of America: trading +70% to $3.62B (a company record), profit +27% to $9.1B. Citigroup: profit $5.8B and equities trading +45% — yet shares fell 5.27% on the gap to larger rivals. Wells Fargo: +17% to $6.4B
  • The engines: SpaceX's $85B IPO drew retail in droves and paid the banks ~$500 million, while the repeated opening and closing of Hormuz, plus the AI argument, generated the volatility they trade on
  • ⚠️ Dimon's warning: praising the economy's "resiliency" and saying conditions are nearly "as good as it gets," he added that "several risks are shifting below the surface like tectonic plates — geopolitical tensions and wars, sticky inflation, large global fiscal deficits, and elevated asset prices"
  • Solomon said the industry is "in the middle of an AI capex super cycle" — Anthropic's IPO will be led by Goldman and Morgan Stanley; OpenAI is still choosing
  • The structure: in Q1 alone, the race to build AI infrastructure pushed combined net equity and bond issuance to a record annualized $3.24 trillion. Banks are returning to the classic way to profit — lending into that capex
💡 Big-picture link

That "AI is scary and there's a war on" made this the best quarter ever for banks tells you what this phase is: what the market is selling isn't a direction, it's volatility. And when Dimon says it's as good as it gets, it pays to listen.

Memory vs Software
(7/14) IBM −25.2%, its worst day ever · the same day SK Hynix's ADR rose 27.3% — "no, this can't be healthy"
Bloomberg · John Authers / Brew Markets · Morning Brew ▾
IBM fell 25.21% — its worst day since at least 1968. The reason is the point: customers redirected budgets to chips, servers and memory, leaving nothing for IBM's software and z17 mainframes. Infrastructure revenue fell 7%. CEO Krishna: these conditions require perfect execution, "and this quarter we faltered."
  • The contagion: Microsoft −1.55%, Workday −3.49%, Salesforce −2.14%, with Accenture and Cognizant lower too — memory inflation is now eating enterprise IT budgets. June 24's "SaaSpocalypse" confirmed in earnings
  • An extra twist: IBM blamed Anthropic's release of Mythos, which pushed companies to spend on cyber defenses amid fears the model could help hackers
  • The mirror image: SK Hynix's ADR rose 27.29% as retail money poured into its new leveraged ETFs. Authers notes that's roughly double IBM's best-ever daily gain — and Hynix is now worth more than IBM ever was
  • The madness in numbers: Hynix's Korean market cap fell $504B in three weeks, then regained $56B on Tuesday — and remains $603B higher than on Jan 1. On Monday it dropped 15% in Seoul on profit-taking, dragging US memory names down with it (SanDisk −12.63%, Western Digital −4.64%, Micron −4.32%)
  • Authers' verdict: "No, this can't be healthy." And his aside: it's no surprise banks are making money out of equity trading
💡 Big-picture link

−25% and +27% on the same day. It's the finished form of June 24's "SaaSpocalypse vs the memory supercycle": AI isn't growing the pie, it's moving it. And once that move runs through leveraged ETFs, the amplitude dwarfs the underlying change.

Apple vs OpenAI / CapEx
(7/13) Apple became AI's unexpected winner — suing OpenAI, and leading the Mag 7 on money it didn't spend
Morning Brew · Tech Brew · Brew Markets ▾
Mocked all year as AI's laggard, Apple hit a record intraday high as the best-performing Magnificent Seven stock of 2026 (+16.72%). The reason is paradoxical: it didn't spend on AI. While rivals poured tens of billions into infrastructure, Apple preserved cash and is expected to generate a record $140 billion of free cash flow this year (+40% vs 2025) — even as Alphabet's free cash flow is expected to plunge by about two-thirds.
  • The core business helps: revenue is seen growing 15% in fiscal 2026 (its fastest since 2021), and Apple raised production targets for its first foldable iPhone (September) from 7–8 million to about 10 million
  • Apple's counterattack: on Friday it sued OpenAI for trade-secret theft. Central to the case is Tang Tan, a former Apple VP and now OpenAI's chief hardware officer, accused of having Apple staff bring actual parts to interviews as "show and tell." Apple says 400+ former employees now work at OpenAI; one allegedly messaged a colleague "LOL, I found out I can access the [network storage], so funny" before downloading design and manufacturing documents. Apple calls OpenAI's hardware business "rotten to its core"; OpenAI denies it: "no interest in other companies' trade secrets"
  • The bill on the other side: Meta raised its Louisiana AI campus from $10B to $50B (doubling compute to 14GW next year) and shares fell 1.86%. Its 2026 capex is already $125–145B. JPMorgan stayed Neutral: Muse Spark is encouraging, but adoption hasn't yet justified the rapidly growing AI bill
  • Big Tech has issued ~$244B of bonds this year (Alphabet, Amazon, Meta, Oracle, Nvidia, SpaceX) — more than double last year's $108B. The race is raising borrowing costs, and the bonds no longer sell like they used to
💡 Big-picture link

Three months ago, "Apple has no AI" was a weakness. Now the money it didn't spend is the earnings — proof the scoring system flipped from "who bets biggest" to "who can carry the bill." It's Authers' July 7 line — all the hyperscalers' free cash now goes to capex — showing up as a leaderboard.

Data Centers
(7/14–15) New York becomes the first state to freeze big new data centers for a year — AI's bill arrives as an electricity bill
Morning Brew · Tech Brew ▾
New York became the first US state to pause approvals for large new data centers for up to a year (an executive order from Governor Hochul). It covers anything drawing 50 megawatts or more — effectively every behemoth proposed lately — while smaller facilities serving hospitals and the like can proceed, as can already-approved projects.
  • The purpose: time for regulators to study whether these facilities raise local power bills, strain water supply or create noise. The governor wants large centers to generate their own electricity or pay extra for grid access, invest in grid infrastructure, and lose tax subsidies
  • It's global: Ireland's data centers consume 23% of national power (nearly as much as all homes); Denmark paused all new large connections with 14GW queued (double its peak demand); in Chile, servers take 62% of one drought-stricken region's power; and Google, Amazon and Microsoft together emit about a third of France's CO2
  • The cost: data centers will add $23 billion to US consumer electricity bills through 2028, and local pushback disrupted $130 billion of US projects in Q1 2026 alone (FT)
  • And yet: Meta more than doubled its Louisiana power capacity from 2GW to 5GW — Big Tech is still winning the war
💡 Big-picture link

Since Q4 2025 the AI argument has had three axes — circular deals, depreciation and power. Power just became a legal constraint for the first time. You can raise infinite capital; you can't manufacture electricity. Authers' "ambition reconciled with physical constraints," delivered as an executive order.

Japan / Flows
(7/13) Japan tells its $1.6 trillion pension fund to bring the money home — the weaponization of investment flows
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Finance Minister Satsuki Katayama announced on Friday that Japan will take measures to make its big money managers invest more at home — explicitly including the $1.6 trillion GPIF. The bond market reacted instantly: the 10-year JGB fell more than at any time since last year's Liberation Day shock.
  • The setup: Japanese yields had run far ahead of the world. Its 30-year yield exceeds even German bunds, despite the ECB having hiked far more aggressively. Blame the world's heaviest debt load — and frustration at the BOJ's slow tightening. SocGen's Albert Edwards: markets are like a football crowd chanting at their own manager, "You don't know what you're doing!"
  • The GPIF contributed: the weighted average maturity of its top 15 holdings fell from 9.6 years in March 2024 to 8.5, then to 7.3 years this March — pushing long yields up
  • For the yen: Deutsche Bank's Saravelos says strength requires either the BOJ hiking to 2% far faster than priced (swaps see 1.2% at year-end) or a concerted push to move domestic investment onshore. SpectraMarkets' Brent Donnelly: "You know who controls the Japanese pension funds? The Japanese government. This is probably a watershed moment for the yen" — though more a slow turning of the ship
  • The bigger frame: the steady weaponization of investment flows as globalization retreats. The UK is trying the same, and a fracturing NATO plus European rearmament means fewer European funds flow to the US — which matters when America is two-thirds of global market cap: downward pressure on the dollar and US assets
💡 Big-picture link

Tariffs (2025) → equity stakes (Intel) → now capital flows. As countries decide their savings stay home, the global savings glut that underwrote America's asset premium gets thinner — arriving precisely when AI capex needs the bond market most.

July 10–12, 2026
Fri–Sun — SK Hynix lists into a bear market, and "the next move is up"
Jul 10
Korea / IPO
(7/10) SK Hynix debuts on the Nasdaq +12.76% — raising $26.5B in America the same week its home market fell into a bear market
Morning Brew · Brew Markets · Finimize ▾
SK Hynix, a $1 trillion memory chipmaker, listed ADRs on the Nasdaq, jumping 12.76% to $168.01 and raising roughly $26.5 billion — the largest ADR offering in market history and the third-largest listing of any kind on record. Preliminary orders exceeded supply sevenfold. Two days after the Kospi entered a bear market, American investors queued up to buy memory.
  • The earnings behind it: high-bandwidth memory demand pushed Q1 2026 profits up almost fivefold, and the stock is up 600% in a year. Analysts expect the memory crunch to persist until at least 2027
  • Use of proceeds: about $28B of aggressive expansion in South Korea and the US, including a fab in Indiana — though analysts warn new plants may take years to produce chips
  • Positioning: a US listing gives it an edge over Samsung (Korea-only), at a cheaper valuation than Micron while holding a larger share of DRAM
  • ⚠️ The catch is leveraged ETFs: they've already pulled in billions in Korea, and at least six US versions launch next week. That machinery helps explain why Hynix's Seoul shares have swung more than 5% in a single day over 50 times this year
  • Same week: the Kospi fell 20% from its June high into a bear market (Jul 8). Even after Samsung reported Q2 profit 19 times higher than a year ago, investors sold — either profit-taking after a 130% run, or real skepticism about the longevity of AI spending
💡 Big-picture link

A bear market on Jul 8, a record ADR two days later. It looks contradictory but it's one story: Korean retail sells, American money buys. The "reliant on retail while foreigners exit" problem Authers named on Jul 1 is settling — in the opposite direction. And remembering that a leveraged ETF turned −13.2% into −26.4% on Jun 24, six US launches plant the same device in a much bigger market.

Iran / Oil
(7/10–11) Khamenei buried, oil +6% to $75 · Trump says the truce is "over" but talks continue · "1000 Missiles" if he's assassinated
Morning Brew · Finimize ▾
Ayatollah Khamenei was buried in his hometown of Mashhad, concluding the funeral processions. The US and Iran are expected to open talks toward a final deal now that they're over. Trump called the ceasefire "over" after this week's incidents — while also saying he'd let negotiations continue. Pakistan's army chief has emerged as a principal mediator.
  • The week in full: Iran attacked tankers in the Strait of Hormuz on Tuesday → the US retaliated with fresh strikes and renewed sanctions on Iranian oil → Brent rose nearly 6% on the week to $75, still far below April's $126 peak
  • Jul 11: citing threats against his life, Trump said "1000 Missiles" would be launched if he were assassinated; some at Khamenei's procession carried "Kill Trump" signs
  • Logistics: Maersk will resume Middle East–US East Coast service via the Red Sea and Suez with US-flagged ships — routing around the Hormuz bottleneck
  • Oil actually drifted lower into the weekend as hopes for continued negotiation won out
💡 Big-picture link

"The truce is over" and "talks continue" from the same mouth — Authers' Memorandum of Misunderstanding and its margin of error, still operating. The market now reads that ambiguity both ways: oil oscillates, and the Fed can't move.

Fed / Rotation
(7/10) Wall Street's new consensus: at least one hike this year, maybe three — yet small caps just had their best half since 1991
Brew Markets · Morning Brew ▾
With oil pushing inflation up, most of Wall Street now expects the Fed to raise rates at least once, and possibly three times, this year — a complete reversal from the "cuts plus AI" consensus of six months ago. And yet the year's winners aren't megacap tech. They're small caps.
  • The Russell 2000 is up 19.16% vs the S&P 500's 10.66% — small caps' best first half since 1991. As the Mag 7 disappointed, money went further afield into quantum computing, nuclear and AI names (five of the Russell 2000's ten best performers were semis or semi equipment)
  • ⚠️ Two reasons it may not last: ① June's rebalance sent 43 stocks up to the Russell 1000 — the best performers leave the index ② hikes hurt small caps most, since they lean harder on borrowing
  • The real economy is cooling: June existing-home sales fell 2.4% (vs +0.7% expected) while the median price hit a record $440,600. The war has pushed mortgage rates back up, killing a decent spring; 2026 sales forecasts were trimmed to 4.10 million
  • Yet earnings expectations are high — the S&P 500 is tracking 23.3% profit growth (a second straight 20%+ quarter, a seventh straight double-digit one)
💡 Big-picture link

The report's thesis completes itself: a market that expected cuts now fears hikes — while profits grow 23%. The problem was never earnings; it's the discount rate. Which makes the most vulnerable asset the one most dependent on debt: small caps, the winner of the first half.

Crypto / Banking
(7/10) Circle just walked into the US banking system — an OCC trust charter, and the banks warn deposits will walk out
Brew Markets · Finimize ▾
Stablecoin issuer Circle won OCC approval to operate as Circle National Trust Bank (shares +4.99%). It still can't take deposits or make loans, but it can now manage the reserves backing USDC directly and answer to a single federal regulator instead of a state-by-state patchwork.
  • The banks' objection: stablecoins compete with deposits, their most valuable asset. If yield is allowed, they warn, consumer, small-business and farm lending could fall more than 20% and community-bank lending alone by up to $850 billion — the fight is over the CLARITY Act's loyalty and membership-reward loophole
  • The counterattack: the largest US banks plan a tokenized deposit network next year, while Citigroup and BNY already run live blockchain platforms
  • The queue: Coinbase, BitGo, Fidelity Digital Assets, Ripple and Paxos have all applied to the OCC
  • The irony: bitcoin is down 27% this year as money rotates into AI and tech — enthusiasm has faded even as institutional integration advances
💡 Big-picture link

The first destination of the road the GENIUS Act built in July 2025. The point is that price (bitcoin −27%) and plumbing (a bank charter) are moving in opposite directions: crypto is turning from a speculative asset into payment infrastructure — which is precisely why Warsh's hawkishness kills one half and spares the other.

AI Landscape
(7/10–11) Meta's Muse Spark 1.1 competes on price · OpenAI ships GPT-5.6 · Apple sues OpenAI · Beijing forces Meta's Manus deal to unwind
Tech Brew · Morning Brew · Finimize ▾
The AI race has narrowed to coding and price. Meta unveiled Muse Spark 1.1, claiming coding performance "improved substantially," in a bid to catch Anthropic and OpenAI — with Zuckerberg promising pricing that is "very aggressive and attractive" and calling rivals' costs "very extreme." The same week, OpenAI released GPT-5.6 and Anthropic shipped a Spotify-Wrapped-style recap for Claude.
  • Apple sued OpenAI, accusing it of stealing trade secrets and confidential information — partners turned litigants
  • Beijing ordered Meta's $2B acquisition of Manus (an AI agent startup) reversed, with Tencent in talks to lead a replacement $2B deal (FT) — AI M&A as a hostage of geopolitics
  • A reversal in the cost debate: Meta rose 5.97% after BofA argued AI infrastructure costs could come in below Wall Street forecasts — the first serious counter to the capex fear that ruled June
  • The borrowing continues: Amazon is selling $25B of bonds (on top of $54B in March) and Nvidia raised $25B in debt — SpaceX, Oracle and Meta run the same playbook
  • US equity funds took in nearly $25B, their biggest inflow in three weeks, as AI earnings expectations improved
💡 Big-picture link

Here's the cause of the token-spend slump Authers flagged on Jul 7. Meta declaring it will compete on price is the race to the bottom in model pricing: usage can rise while revenue is eaten by falling unit prices — and meanwhile the infrastructure debt ($25B at a time) keeps stacking. The physical version of "the vertical ascent is over."

July 9, 2026
Thursday — Truce over · AI repricing
Jul 9
War / Energy
Trump declares the Iran truce over — Brent surges anew
Bloomberg (John Authers) / Finimize ▾
June's "Memorandum of Understanding" truce is officially dead. Brent jumped 6% — the most since April 29 — to $78.02, up 11.6% for the week. Authers dubbed it a "Memorandum of Misunderstanding": markets had overread a fragile pause as a virtual peace treaty.
  • Note the round trip: during the June truce, oil had fallen so far that "imminent glut" talk emerged and inflation swaps returned to prewar (Feb 27) levels
  • Warsh's first FOMC minutes (released Jul 8) were genuinely hawkish: a few members saw a case for a hike last month; the minutes cite "strong AI-related demand," the Middle East, and tariffs as reasons policy "firming would likely be warranted"
  • Trump in Ankara: a ground war would be a "waste of time" — escalation risk stays slim; the market reaction is jaded, not shocked
  • EM hit instantly: rupee at a one-month low, EM FX index falling again. Capital Economics' best case: a messy ceasefire with oil near $80 (still +30% YTD)
💡 Big picture

Q2's rally rested on two pillars — truce hopes (oil↓, inflation↓) and the AI supercycle. The first collapsed today; the second is wobbling the same week.

AI / Market
"Like AI never happened" — why Nvidia suddenly looks cheap
Morning Brew / Brew Markets ▾
Morning Brew's headline says it all. The $5T champion of 14 straight record quarters is now the subject of a "does it look cheap?" debate as megacap AI names correct broadly. Nvidia had already lagged the SOX by 40+ points YTD in May — the seeds were planted early.
  • June's accumulating weight: Anthropic export freeze, Alphabet share sale, Nvidia joining the borrowing wave
  • Late-June Mag 7 rotation → full repricing in early July, mirrored by Korea's bear market (Jul 8)
Geopolitics / Endgame
(7/9) Authers: "The Memorandum of Misunderstanding's margin of error" — what was called a truce was never an agreement
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Trump declared the truce with Iran is over, and Brent responded. Authers gives the episode a brutal name: it was never a Memorandum of Understanding but a Memorandum of Misunderstanding — and the problem was its margin of error. If each side believed it had signed something different, the collapse was only a matter of time.
  • The market's misreading: it priced the June 29 ceasefire as a resolution, when it was a document that preserved ambiguity. July 9's rupture was inside that margin of error from the start
  • The result: four and a half months after the war began on Feb 27, Hormuz is still unresolved and oil points up again. The chain of "peace → cooler inflation → hopes of easing" reverses wholesale
💡 Big-picture link

The report's terminus. Korea −20% on July 8 plus the ceasefire's collapse and Brent's spike on July 9 broke both narratives that ruled the first half — endless AI ascent and imminent peace — within two days. The end of a half-year that went from expecting cuts to fearing hikes.

July 8, 2026
Wednesday — Korea in bear country
Jul 8
Market
South Korea's dip takes it to bear country — the +78% YTD Kospi is down 20% from its peak
Finimize / Brew Markets ▾
The world's hottest market of May — +78% YTD, 7th largest globally at $4.59T, Samsung in the $1T club — has fallen more than 20% from its peak, entering an official bear market. Samsung's disappointing update (Jul 7) was the trigger; the memory-supercycle crowding is unwinding. Energy prices rose again the same day as truce doubts resurfaced.
Iran / Oil
(7/8) "Iran can't help gloating, and oil prices go up" — the US launches a new wave of strikes
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
On the very day Korea entered a bear market, geopolitics broke too: the US launched a new wave of military strikes on Iran, barely a week after the ceasefire. Authers' read: Iran couldn't resist gloating about its grip on the waterway — and that triggered the re-escalation. Oil turned back up.
  • June 29 ceasefire → July 8 strikes: the peace premium evaporated in ten days, unwinding everything markets had priced by "looking past" the truce
  • The cruelty of the timing: as an over-levered Kospi was already breaking, rising oil = inflation reignites = no Fed cut — leaving no cushion anywhere
💡 Big-picture link

The moment June's "AI masks the war" mask lifts. AI (Korea) and the war (Iran) broke on the same day.

July 6–7, 2026
Mon–Tue
Jul 6–7
Market
SpaceX joins the Nasdaq 100 (Jul 6) · Samsung disappoints (Jul 7) · Amazon returns to the bond market
Finimize ▾
Less than a month after listing, SpaceX entered the Nasdaq 100 — the pre-arranged fast-track index inclusion (and its army of passive buyers) executed on schedule, while the S&P 500 still holds out. Samsung "still couldn't please investors," and Amazon issued bonds to fund AI capex, extending the financing rush after Alphabet's share sale and Nvidia's borrowing.
AI Economics
(7/7) Authers: "A token grasp of the AI boom shows trouble brewing" — the period of vertical ascent is probably over
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Chip stocks rebounded after the long American weekend, helped by Samsung's profits rising 19-fold. But Authers applies the boom's most honest thermometer — token spending. The Silicon Data Token Expenditure Index has plunged from record highs, offering "signs of trouble brewing beneath the surface."
  • The index blends price and usage, so the dip could mean any of three things: falling list prices, a shift toward cheaper models (notably Chinese ones), or softening willingness to pay. Only weeks ago it was double late last year's level — even though a single token's price has fallen over 90% since 2023
  • The hyperscalers have changed character (BofA's Savita Subramanian): after two years of the AI arms race, virtually all the free cash they produce now goes to capex — meaning chips. Once investors grasped these firms depend on capital as much as old-line industrials — erasing tech's decades-long advantage — their shares stalled. Microsoft is 28% below its October high
  • Morgan Stanley's Michael Wilson: "I expect the hyperscalers to stabilize… and the semiconductor stocks are going to correct. That's a good development. You can't have this divergence continue"
  • The long-run case survives: BCG finds tokens multiply knowledge work much as the assembly line did for manufacturing. But Citadel's Frank Flight cautions the key variable isn't productivity — it's the elasticity of demand for the tasks whose costs are falling; markets shouldn't anchor on a world where AI is ubiquitous and frictionless
  • The conclusion: expect uneven diffusion — frontier deployment where returns justify the compute, with asset prices periodically forced to reconcile ambition with physical constraints. Put differently, "the period of vertical ascent is probably over."
💡 Big-picture link

One day before the break, the diagnosis isn't "AI is over" but "the vertical ascent is over." The next day, Korea proves it with −20%.

June 29 – July 3, 2026
Mon–Fri — the best quarter since 2020, and a warning
Wk of Jun 29
Bubble Watch / H1 Review
(7/1) Authers: "In hindsight, will a coming crash seem obvious?" — a first half that rhymes with July 2008
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
The first half of 2026 is in the books: chipmakers just had their best-ever quarter, and the S&P 500 its best since 2020. Authers pulled forward his Hindsight Capital review — the imaginary fund that invests with perfect foreknowledge — six months early. Why? Because "with another six months' hindsight, we may think it was always obvious a crash was coming."
  • The 2008 template: in that year's first half, the winning trade was shorting banks and buying oil — it more than doubled your money. Then in early July it collapsed under its own weight: if the banking system was imploding, oil demand couldn't hold records. By year-end, bank stocks had beaten oil. "Is this the same juncture?"
  • Best trade of the half: short the S&P 1500 software index, buy the SOX = +150%. Korea was more extreme still — the Kospi has nearly doubled this year; pairing long Kospi with short Jakarta trebled your money in six months
  • Hard money broke: long Brent (+19.8%) / short bitcoin (−33.3%) = +79.4%; long gasoline futures (+76.7%) / short silver (−18.3%) = +116%. The war made oil the more reliable hard asset, and then Warsh arrived as a genuine hawk — sinking the debasement trade
  • The economic limit (BCA's Budaghyan): in the US, capex on everything except tech is falling. Tech capex passed its 2000 peak of 5% of GDP and is now above 7% in real terms — the very level at which housing bubbles burst in the US (7%), Japan (9%) and Spain (8%). "AI is beginning to suck in capital others cannot spare"; the argument that current trends have only months to run looks reasonable
  • Speculative excess: big IPOs historically arrive at sector tops. And Korea's amazing advance happened despite an exodus of foreign capital — leaving the Kospi "alarmingly reliant on the exuberance of Korean retail investors." "It's hard to imagine that extreme rally lasting another six months without reversing — but we don't yet have hindsight"
💡 Big-picture link

One week after this piece, Korea's −20% bear market arrives on July 8. The three things Authers named — a retail-dependent Kospi, AI capex overshoot, and the July-2008 pattern — play out in exactly that order.

Iran / Ceasefire
(6/29) US and Iran agree a ceasefire — but markets are already looking past it. Then what?
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Iran and the US agreed to stop firing at each other, and oil fell on hopes the four-month war was ending. But as Authers' headline implies, markets were already looking past the ceasefire — the question was what comes next.
  • A ceasefire isn't peace — it's a halt to shooting. Hormuz normalization, sanctions and the nuclear file remain unresolved, and the June 19 framework had already been canceled at the last minute
  • The paradox: cheaper oil eases inflation, but the reason for Warsh to stay hawkish (solid growth) is untouched → still no cuts
💡 Big-picture link

This is the very ceasefire Trump declares over on July 9. The peace markets had priced by "looking past it" evaporates in ten days.

Fed Independence
(6/30) SCOTUS: the president can't fire Fed governors — Trump lost the battle, but look what he won
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
The Supreme Court blocked the firing of a Fed governor. On its face, a defeat for Trump and a win for Fed independence. But Authers reframes it: Trump lost the battle — look at what he won.
  • What he won: he already installed his own pick in the chair (Warsh). You can remake the Fed through appointments without any power to fire — the irony being that this Warsh turned out to be a hawk who refuses to cut
  • Market implication: the path to forcing rates down by political pressure is now legally closed too — the last basis for "cut expectations" is gone
Yield Curve
(7/2) "Warsh must beware of curves flattening to deceive" — he claims he's winning so far
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Warsh claims he's winning so far — inflation risks are receding thanks to his hawkishness. Authers' caution: a flattening yield curve can deceive. Long rates sinking may say "inflation is beaten" — or it may say "growth is cracking."
  • The same week's data pulled both ways: JOLTS vacancies returned to their pre-pandemic peak (strong) ↔ Friday's jobs report disappointed (weak)
  • Warsh's dilemma: stay hawkish into a growth crack, and the curve's "victory signal" turns out to have been a warning
💡 Big-picture link

"Inflation cooling while growth cracks" — the sketch of why, in July's selloff, the Fed can't come to the rescue.

Quarter End
(7/1–3) The best quarter since 2020 — then Friday's jobs report disappoints
Morning Brew ▾
On the half's final trading day, the S&P 500 booked its best quarter since 2020 ("Firework") — a half-year that survived a war, 4% inflation and a hawkish Fed because AI lifted everything. Then the tone began to shift as the July 3 jobs report disappointed.
  • China's AI is catching up to stalled-out US models (6/29) — the "Two Silicon Valleys" framing
  • 7/1: Anthropic unveils Claude Fable 5; Meta starts renting out spare cloud capacity — an attempt to monetize capex overshoot
  • Trump says he made $1.2 billion from crypto businesses last year — reigniting the policy/asset conflict-of-interest debate
June 22–26, 2026
Mon–Fri — the tremor that came from Korea
Wk of Jun 22
Korea / Bubble Watch
(6/24) "It came from Korea" — one remark from SK Hynix, a 13.2% drop, and global tech shakes
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Tech sold off hard — and it couldn't be blamed on geopolitics (the Middle East was calming, oil falling). The spark came from Korea. SK Hynix, another freshly minted trillion-dollar chipmaker, announced nothing significant; it merely said it was "shifting its focus to the general-purpose DRAM market." Memory is less profitable → maybe it isn't quite the license to print money traders assumed → −13.2% in a day. Authers: "Is this the moment the bubble bursts? Probably not, but it's a mighty interesting juncture."
  • There was room to rethink because Hynix's run had been so breakneck it makes even the 2000 dot-com bubble pale by comparison
  • The real risk is leveraged single-stock ETFs: holders of Hong Kong's CSOP double-leveraged SK Hynix ETF saw −13.2% become −26.4%. Korean regulators, who recently allowed single-company ETFs, publicly regretted it this week — spooking the market further
  • Contagion: US momentum stocks took the hit — the S&P 500 momentum ETF had its second-worst session since last year's Liberation Day. Even after the drop, the SOX sits far above its 200-day average, more overbought than at any time since the dot-com peak
  • Authers: "Good news — so far this isn't even a correction. Worse news — that leaves much space for over-levered investors to suffer losses they can't afford." Even if the AI story is right, an over-extended, speculative market opens the risk of an accident
  • Collateral damage, India: the war's most innocent bystander (energy importer next to the war zone). GDP grew 7.7%, yet its MSCI EM weight collapsed from 20% in 2024 to under 12% — "a disconnect of this magnitude doesn't come along often" (Barclays)
💡 Big-picture link

The clear precursor to Korea's July 8 bear market (−20%). That a non-event remark could produce −13.2% was itself the tremor revealing how fragile prices had already become.

Software vs Semis
(6/24) The "SaaSpocalypse" — software has shed a third in eight months as capital pours into AI infrastructure
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Even as investors fretted about chips, the software "SaaSpocalypse" ground on. The S&P 1500 Software Index has lost nearly a third of its value in eight months — weakness that predates the Iran conflict.
  • The cause (Apollo's Torsten Slok): ① AI disrupting incumbents' business models ② higher-for-longer rates straining the debt that financed them. Direct lending to SaaS firms exploded from $8B in 2015 to $538B in 2025, leaving enormous capital exposed to both forces
  • Concerns over Anthropic's Mythos and Fable models, which prompted US export restrictions, add to the fears
  • But it isn't evaporation: flows crowding into AI capex plays mean software loses both capital and attention (Morningstar). D.E. Shaw finds productivity growth above 5% in the industries most exposed to AI — roughly double the economy-wide pace — so long term the sector could even benefit
  • The bull case for chips: BofA raised its US chip demand forecast to $2.7 trillion by 2030 (from $2.3T), implying 28% annual growth — "memory shortages and price inflation remain the critical moving pieces"
💡 Big-picture link

The AI rally was never a rising tide — it was a zero-sum rotation from software into infrastructure. The more extreme the crowding, the more one wobble shakes everything: the structural backdrop to July.

Fed / Debasement Trade
(6/25) How Warsh sank the debasement trade — while Micron calmed the chip fear
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
A day after the tremor from Korea, all eyes turned to Micron (now a $1.2 trillion company) and its fiscal third-quarter results — where forecast EPS had risen 645% in 12 months, an expectations bar through the roof. The verdict: profits are growing even faster than thought, and the memory supercycle narrative held.
  • At the same time, Authers argued Warsh sank the "debasement trade" — his hawkish turn lifted real yields and the dollar, undermining the logic of buying gold and bitcoin against a debased currency
  • Bitcoin: the massive rally that followed institutions persuading the SEC to license spot ETFs is now "well and truly over" (Authers) — a structure that depended on drawing in ever more new buyers
  • So late June split in two: fundamentals (Micron) held, while the monetary/liquidity narrative (gold, crypto) broke on Warsh
💡 Big-picture link

Good earnings don't save you when the discount rate (real yields) rises. In July that combination — strong results, a hawkish Fed, and geopolitics returning — converges in its worst form.

Fed History
(6/23) Alan Greenspan dies at 100 — the era of the "Greenspan put" crosses paths with Warsh's
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
The Fed's long-time Maestro, Alan Greenspan, has died at 100. Authers' verdict: his legacy is as convoluted as his words — the chairman who spoke in deliberate obscurity, the author of the "Greenspan put" that promised a rescue whenever markets wobbled, and the man who warned of "irrational exuberance" yet couldn't stop the bubble.
  • The timing is pointed: the symbol of an era of many words and reliable rescues departs in the very week of Warsh — who says little (341→130 words) and won't rescue anyone
  • Every overheating signal this week — SK Hynix, SpaceX, bitcoin ETFs — is now a test of how it all ends without a Greenspan put
Consumer / AI Cost
(6/26) "iFlation" — Apple raises prices on AI inflation · OpenAI kicks the IPO can
Morning Brew · Tech Brew ▾
The cost of the AI boom finally reached the consumer price tag. Apple raised prices across a range of products — because of AI inflation, i.e. surging component (memory) costs. Morning Brew's word for it: "iFlation."
  • The causal loop closes: AI datacenter demand → memory shortages and price spikes (the very source of Hynix's and Micron's earnings) → higher device prices → consumer inflation → Warsh's vigilance
  • The same day, OpenAI delayed its IPO ("kicks the can") — refusing to rush even in a market where SpaceX hit $2.75T in three days
  • Prediction markets (Polymarket, Kalshi) are booming, but hacks and fake bets are multiplying alongside
💡 Big-picture link

Contrary to the "AI is deflationary" assumption, at this stage AI was a cause of inflation — pointing the same way as the June 11 small-business price warning, and stacking one more reason the Fed can't cut.

June 15–19, 2026
Mon–Fri — ⚡ The pivot week
Wk of Jun 15
War / Energy
US–Iran agree an interim peace framework — then Friday's talks are canceled at the last minute
Finimize ▾
A framework that would have reopened the Strait of Hormuz — the chokepoint carrying roughly a fifth of the world's oil — pulled crude lower, exactly what the Fed wanted to see. Then Friday's cancellation revived every doubt, foreshadowing July's collapse.
Fed / BOJ / AI
Fed holds but the dots shift UP · BOJ hikes to highest since 1995 · US orders Anthropic freeze for non-citizens
Finimize ▾
  • Fed: held at 3.50–3.75%, but the dot plot now says the next move is more likely a hike. May retail sales +0.9% (double forecasts) backed the hawks
  • BOJ: highest rates since 1995 — carry-trade unwind risk goes global
  • Anthropic: ordered to switch off its most powerful models for all non-US citizens (they're too good at finding software flaws). "Anthropic's freeze could chill the whole AI trade"
  • China: retail sales fell for the first time in 3+ years (cars -16%) while factories hummed — the two-tier economy deepens. Nvidia joined the AI borrowing wave
Fed / Deep-dive
(6/17) Authers deep-dive — Warsh, the "strong silent hawk": FOMC statement cut 341→130 words, and he wouldn't even leave his own dot
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
The message was in the form, not the substance of the hold (12–0, 3.50–3.75%). Warsh cut the FOMC statement from 341 words to 130 and refused to leave even his own dot on the dot plot. Authers' reading: it's the end of forward guidance, and the very brevity is a strong hawkish signal — "there's nothing there to open any excuse to cut."
  • Markets read it as a hawkish surprise: the 10-year TIPS real yield jumped to its highest since last May, and the dollar had its strongest rise of the year (unwelcome for an administration that wanted peace to weaken it)
  • Warsh withheld his own dot, but his 18 colleagues' dots moved sharply toward hikes — the average year-end projection rose from 3.24% to 3.83%
  • Five task forces announced (communications, balance sheet, alternative data, productivity & AI, the inflation framework). But the 2% target stays — he rejects the 3% target Trump wanted (which would ease cuts)
  • Falling oil handed Warsh a chance to duck and be dovish; he took the hawkish path instead — "we'll all have to get used to more surprises like this" (Authers)
💡 Big-picture link

The core of the report's ⚡ pivot. The "expecting cuts → fearing hikes" shift that had built since late May hardened into "the next move is up" at Warsh's very first meeting.

SpaceX / Bubble Watch
(6/17) SpaceX is worth $2.75 trillion three days after listing — leapfrogging Amazon
Bloomberg · John Authers / Morning Brew ▾
After its June 12 debut, SpaceX soared to a $2.75 trillion market value in just three days, overtaking Amazon (nearing "$3 trillion in three days"). Retail piled in, but Authers — under the headline "bad gnus" — flagged the valuation as overheated.
  • From a $1.78T IPO value to +50% in three days — a live test of how much the market will pay for a loss-making company (~$5B in losses)
  • It's the valuation benchmark for the OpenAI and Anthropic listings, and its portfolio-crowding effect adds to volatility in other megacaps
💡 Big-picture link

The largest IPO ever ballooning to a mega-valuation within days — the peak of crowding, and fuel for July's repricing.

Iran / Global Tightening
(6/16) Authers: "the two clear winners are Iran and markets" — and global rates diverge
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
In the week the Hormuz-reopening deal was expected to be signed on Friday, Authers argued the war's "two clear winners are Iran and markets" — the regime is intact and has proven its grip on the waterway, while markets rallied on cheaper oil. (The framework is then canceled at the last minute on Friday.)
  • Global rates diverge: after the Fed's hawkish turn and an ECB hike, the UK (BOE) now sees just one hike this year (down from three) as inflation and oil cool — UK core CPI has slipped below the US
  • The G7 is pushing to cut its dependence on Chinese rare earths — war and supply chains spilling into national security
  • Shuli Ren (BB Opinion): "this $1 trillion Kospi whale has made a grave mistake" — a warning on Korea's crowding also lands this week
💡 Big-picture link

Peace hopes crack at the peak (Friday's cancellation) as major central banks tilt hawkish together. The taut balance just before June's mask lifts into July's collapse.

June 10–11, 2026
Wed–Thu — 4% inflation, yet a very calm market
Week 2
Inflation / Fed / ECB
(6/11) CPI tops 4% — yet the market is a bit too relaxed. Fed likely to hold; ECB hikes
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
US consumer prices topped 4% for the first time in three years, yet the market barely flinched. Authers' read: energy is a big part of the problem (so "Iran news matters more than backward-looking data"), and core (ex food and energy) rose but came in below expectations. Above all, the print wasn't bad enough to put a hike on the table at Warsh's first FOMC — a hold is now virtually certain. Market-based inflation expectations (swaps, breakevens) keep falling, treating the Iran shock as transitory, like tariffs before it.
  • But Authers warns the market may be too calm: the Fed's favored "supercore" (services ex-housing) is rising sharply, the Atlanta Fed's sticky-price gauge is above 3%, and both trimmed mean and median top core CPI — firm upward pressure. A cut is ruled out too
  • Conversely, tariff-driven goods inflation is falling (a win for the "tariff fuss was overdone" camp) as last year's tariffs drop out of year-on-year math; it's even getting hard to pin inflation on shelter (CPI ex-shelter is above 4%)
  • The same week, the ECB hikes on Thursday — energy-dependent Europe takes the war's cost squarely on the chin
💡 Big-picture link

A "neither hike nor cut" stalemate is Warsh's dilemma. Pressure building beneath the calm is the fuse for the June dot-plot's "next move is up" and July's reversal.

Small Business / Leading Indicator
(6/11) The real cracks are in small business — NFIB prices index highest since 1981, warning of reacceleration in ~9 months
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
If inflation threatens the US economy, it does so through the small businesses that form its backbone. May's NFIB small-business optimism index came in below expectations and gave up all its gains since Trump was elected, with the Middle East crisis the biggest driver of pessimism.
  • The NFIB prices index is at its highest since 1981 (ex-pandemic). A third have already raised selling prices and another third plan to; 70%+ cite supply-chain disruptions, hiring plans slipped, and capex is at its lowest since 2009
  • SMBC Nikko notes the NFIB prices survey has historically led inflation inflection points by nine months → it now points to a reacceleration
  • Larger firms hold up via scale, capital and faster AI adoption — "despite the AI enthusiasm, the picture is divided" (NFIB chief economist). Small firms struggle most to pass on fuel costs
💡 Big-picture link

A K-shaped split between big (AI) and small (fuel bills). Cracks growing beneath a record-high index — another seed of summer volatility.

AI CapEx
(6/10) The Oracle shock — a −25% slide on soaring AI datacenter spend, and "where does the money come from?"
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Oracle, remaking itself as an AI hyperscaler, disclosed bigger-than-expected datacenter capex and fell 10% after hours, down 25% from last week's peak. The Nasdaq 100 is −7% from its peak (still +12% YTD) and semis are −12%. The market has finally started asking "where will the money for all this capex come from?"
  • Goldman's Ryan Hammond: 2027 hyperscaler capex consensus of $920B is too conservative — if investment reaches 2–3% of GDP (like the railroad/auto build-outs), it could hit $1.1 trillion (+45%), implying more Oracle-style lurches
  • New AI listings (SpaceX on Friday) also churn things, forcing buyers to make room in their portfolios
💡 Big-picture link

May's "AI peak" → June's "capex bill." The growth story is intact, but financing strain becomes the trigger for repricing — a textbook setup.

AI vs War
(6/11) The AI boom is masking the war — strip out tech and world equities are down since the war began
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
The Iran war has flared to its worst since the ceasefire two months ago, and Hormuz traffic remains minimal — yet stocks and oil react less and less. Authers' key insight: the AI boom is masking the war's impact.
  • Strip out chip-heavy Korea and Taiwan, plus US tech, and major MSCI markets are all down since the war began (Feb 27) — so the war is a far bigger drag than many assume
  • Energy stocks surged in March, but across Trump 2.0 they've done "exactly as well" as the rest of the market — the oil shock has diffused everywhere
  • Authers' warning: escalation in the Middle East, or a tumble in AI capex, would test this mask — a test everyone wants to avoid
💡 Big-picture link

That exact test arrives in July — a ceasefire collapse (Mideast re-escalation) and AI repricing at once. The moment June's mask lifts is the reversal.

June 1–5, 2026
Mon–Fri — a strategic debacle, yet stocks win
Week 1
Geopolitics / Markets
(6/1) Iran war at 3 months — a "strategic debacle" for the US, yet stocks keep winning. Why?
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
The war is three months old. Ten weeks after Trump declared Iran's navy and air force "demolished," Iran is still choking traffic through the Strait of Hormuz (US personnel were hurt in a strike on a Kuwaiti base over the weekend). Authers calls it a "strategic debacle for the US" — the regime is intact and has learned how easily it can close a vital waterway. Yet the market reaction is euphoric.
  • Why the rally: ① the AI-chip boom is critical ② an underlying confidence the Iran situation won't cause serious damage. Absolute Strategy's survey of big asset allocators shows optimism undented
  • More striking, a majority now expect multiples to expand — the first time since the depths of the pandemic. The Shiller CAPE is at its highest since Sept 2000, exceeding even the eve of the 1929 crash; sales multiples are the highest in history
  • Prediction markets have essentially given up on Hormuz reopening in June; long-dated oil futures price a lasting-but-not-catastrophic impact
💡 Big-picture link

This very "it's manageable" optimism was the premise for July's reversal — the gap between fundamentals (a strategic debacle, record valuations) and prices stretched to its widest here.

Inflation / Fed
(6/1) 4% inflation vs. Warsh's room to move — the debate that will dominate H2
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
The war has pushed inflation up — headline prices ran close to 4% in the year to April, and even the long-milder PCE now agrees with CPI. On its face, that's far above the 3% upper band, killing hopes of cuts. But there's a twist.
  • The trimmed-mean PCE is declining (conventional core says the opposite) — and new Chair Warsh follows the trimmed mean, which could open a door to easing later this year, depending on the Middle East
  • Inflation swaps show 1–2yr expectations dropping sharply over two weeks (once +100bps since the war, now half retraced); meanwhile year-end Fed-rate expectations are up 75bps since the war began
  • Asset allocators expect core PCE to rise over the next year — implying hikes, and deep pessimism about a Gulf resolution
💡 Big-picture link

"Cut or hike" ultimately hinges on Hormuz. The June-framework hopes (easing) vs. the July ceasefire collapse (hikes) — that seesaw is foreshadowed right here.

Oil / Iran
(6/1) Oil jumps +5.9% — Iran vows to "completely" block Hormuz, Trump "couldn't care less"
Brew Markets ▾
After Israel's strikes on Lebanon, Iran ended truce talks with the US and vowed to "completely" block the Strait of Hormuz, sending oil up +5.86% ($92.48) in a day. Trump told CNBC he "couldn't care less" if talks end, pushing hopes of a reopening further out. Even so, the S&P, Nasdaq and Dow all hit records on Nvidia-led tech strength.
  • The 10-year yield rose as tensions escalated; all eyes on Friday's jobs report and the Fed's next move
  • US manufacturing activity rose in May at its fastest pace in four years — solid growth coexisting with geopolitical risk
China / Macro
(6/1) Cracks in "invincible China" — the energy crisis seeps in layer by layer
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
The war's fallout isn't just American. Authers argues the Iran-driven energy crisis is puncturing the notion of an invincible China. May's China Beige Book showed weakening revenues and margins, softer consumption (less travel and leisure), and more disappointing housing data — the buffers are showing their limits.
  • Only exports stayed robust (more US-bound goods after tariff easing) — but that's uncertain if the Iran stalemate drags on. Producer prices are the hottest since the pandemic, yet consumer inflation is muted (weak demand)
  • China is drawing down oil inventories → easing near-term oil-price pressure but "worse may lie ahead," potentially pushing the global oil tipping point into Q3
  • Xi keeps avoiding big consumer stimulus; the yuan is at a 3-year high, and AI-linked industrial output hit a 29-month high
💡 Big-picture link

A single chokepoint at Hormuz moves US inflation → the Fed, and Chinese growth → the yuan and oil, all at once. The textbook "one geopolitical point → the whole global macro."

AI PC / Capital Rotation
(6/1) Nvidia's AI laptop, a "40-year reinvention" · capital sorts winners (buy homes vs. bitcoin surrender)
Brew Markets ▾
The AI supercycle is trying to spread from the datacenter to consumer devices. At Computex, Jensen Huang unveiled the RTX Spark superchip + AI laptops built with Microsoft — "the first completely reinvented PC line in 40 years" — running AI agents on-device rather than in the cloud.
  • Winners: Nvidia +6.26%, Arm +15.73%, software (ServiceNow +9.24%, Asana +17.79%, Adobe +5.74%) on the bet that "AI is a demand driver, not a threat." Losers: Intel −4.67%, Qualcomm −8.78%, Apple −1.84% (Mac Mini risk). Analysts caution the Arm-Windows ecosystem needs several product generations
  • Capital sorting winners: ① Berkshire (Abel's first deal) buys homebuilder Taylor Morrison for $6.8B (24% premium) — a contrarian bet amid a rate-hit housing slump; Buffett: "He has launched." ② Strategy (Saylor) sold bitcoin for the first time since 2022 (32 BTC), reversing its "never sell" vow (BTC −43% from its Oct high, Strategy −58%). Betting on the real economy, retreating from speculation
  • Anthropic filed confidentially for an IPO — a step ahead of OpenAI in the three-way mega-IPO race (SpaceX, OpenAI, Anthropic)

Memory / Micron
(5/26) Micron joins the $1T club — up 19%, UBS nearly triples target from $535 to $1,635
Brew Markets ▾
After Samsung and SK Hynix, Micron jumped 19.29% in a day to cross a $1T market cap (the 13th company to do so). Trump touted Micron's plan to spend up to $100B on the largest US chip fab, but the real catalyst was UBS nearly tripling its target from $535 to $1,635 (the highest on Wall Street).
  • UBS's case: the DRAM market is deeply undersupplied; Micron is a top-3 DRAM maker with a low forward P/E, and hyperscalers are locking in long-term deals
  • The memory supercycle is spreading from Korea (Samsung, SK Hynix) to the US (Micron) — all driven by AI demand

Samsung crossed $1T on 5/18; Micron on 5/26. The synchronized memory blow-off marks May's peak — a stark "overheating" signal ahead of the summer.

Economy / Fed
(5/29) CEO confidence craters to 47 · Q1 GDP cut to 1.6% · inflation 3.8% (3-yr high) → 37% odds of a December hike
Morning Brew ▾
Optimism cooled fast. The Conference Board's CEO confidence index fell to 47 in Q2, down 12 points (below 50 is negative). The share of CEOs saying conditions had worsened jumped from 8% to 47%, and 40% expect things to get worse over six months. Consumer confidence also slipped in May (Michigan's gauge at an all-time low).
  • Q1 GDP was revised down from 2% to 1.6% (weaker investment and consumer spending); 12-month inflation hit 3.8%, a three-year high, on high gas prices
  • Fed: Chair Warsh wants to cut but reheating inflation makes it hard — CME puts December hike odds at 37%, as officials doubt AI-fueled growth will cool inflation
💡 Big-picture link

The shift from "expecting cuts" to "fearing hikes" was cemented in late May. Record-high stocks vs. deteriorating fundamentals — the seed of summer volatility.

Brew Markets ▾
Ferrari unveiled its first fully electric car, "Luce" (~$640k), but shares fell 5.26% on fears an EV dilutes the brand. Chips / $1T Club
(5/27) Authers: "A rally for the memory banks" — worst consumer sentiment since 1978, yet a record S&P, entirely on chips
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
"Demand for silicon chips is sky-high, their supply is limited, and that means their share price has gone parabolic." That one line, Authers says, explains why the S&P 500 closed at yet another record despite the worst University of Michigan consumer sentiment reading since the survey began in 1978. Micron jumped 19.3% into the $1 trillion club (now 10 US members), and hours later SK Hynix crossed the same line — Samsung having done so weeks earlier. The biggest chipmakers now rival or surpass Meta.
  • Two catalysts for Micron: ① UBS doubled its target — if right, Micron is worth about $1.8 trillion a year from now ② Huawei proclaimed a breakthrough it calls Logic Folding (China's bid in the AI war) — which, paradoxically, was taken as a reason to pile into US chipmakers
  • Leadership has changed hands: this is a belated correction to the assumption the Magnificent Seven would hog AI's returns — hyperscalers must spend heavily, and much of their projected profit flows to key suppliers. Hindsight Capital's "short Mag 7, buy the SOX" returned 71% this year. Of the S&P's 672-point rally, Mag 7 contributed 160 points and seven SOX chipmakers 289 — strip out Nvidia and it's 79 points for the Mag Six vs 369 for the SOX 8
  • But this isn't a valuation bubble: on a multiple of expected earnings over the next two years, Micron is actually cheaper than the S&P 500. The catch is that cheapness assumes the forward estimates are reasonable — "If these estimates are right, chipmakers will make fortunes. But there's a very good chance that they aren't"
  • Same for EM: strip out its three biggest AI proxies and the group is back where it stood right after last year's Liberation Day. MSCI EM's four largest constituents — China, South Korea, India, Taiwan — are all among those most vulnerable to a prolonged Hormuz closure
💡 Big-picture link

The origin point of Korea's July 8 bear market. The warning that "the estimates may be wrong" runs straight through SK Hynix's −13.2% on June 24, the "retail-dependent Kospi" of July 1, and −20% on July 8.

Oil / Politics
(5/26–28) "Peace — or surrender — in the Strait" · oil −5.6%, and why the market isn't working as Trump's guardrail
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
As hopes of an Iran deal grew, WTI dropped 5.55% to $88.68. But Authers described what was happening at Hormuz as "peace — or surrender." And he posed a larger question: why isn't the market working as Trump's guardrail?
  • Traditionally, bond and equity markets discipline a president's policies (Liz Truss lasted 49 days). But with the AI boom holding prices up, the market can't sound its alarm
  • The result: policy experiments — tariffs, the Middle East, Fed appointments — proceed without a brake, so risk isn't priced. It accumulates instead
💡 Big-picture link

"If the market won't warn, policy won't stop" — the prototype of June's "AI masks the war." The accumulated bill comes due in July.

Utilities / AI Power
(5/18) NextEra to buy Dominion for ~$67B — creating the world's largest regulated utility
Brew Markets ▾
NextEra Energy agreed to buy Dominion Energy for roughly $67B, creating the world's largest regulated electric utility (~$249B cap; third-largest US energy company after Exxon and Chevron). NextEra is the top US renewables developer; Dominion powers the world's largest datacenter market (Northern Virginia). On the day, Dominion +9.44%, NextEra −4.63%.
  • Terms: ~$76/share to Dominion holders + a one-time $360M cash payment + 0.8138 NextEra shares each; NextEra holders end up owning ~75% of the combined company
  • ~10M utility customer accounts across the US East Coast
  • Wedbush (Dan Ives): the clearest confirmation yet that the AI power-demand supercycle is "a decades-long infrastructure build, not a cyclical trade"
  • Gabelli: utilities should grow EPS above historical rates through at least 2030 on datacenter and advanced-manufacturing demand
💡 Big-picture link

"The most boring sector on the board just became the hottest." One of the two pillars of the Q2 rally — the AI supercycle — is spilling into power and grid infrastructure.

Korea / Memory
(5/18) Samsung tops a $1T valuation; SK Hynix +799% — but Samsung's union threatens an 18-day strike
Brew Markets ▾
A deep dive on the Korean side of the memory supercycle. Samsung crossed a $1T valuation (+394% in 12 months); last quarter sales +70% and profit +750%. With ~36% of the global DRAM market, it is the biggest winner of the hyperscaler demand surge. SK Hynix (+799% in a year, ~32% of DRAM) is close to becoming Korea's second $1T company.
  • Shortage deepening: hyperscaler DRAM demand is dramatically outpacing production, driving a surge in orders
  • Risk: ~45,000 Samsung union members announced an 18-day strike from May 21 (talks broke down over demands for 15% of operating profit and no bonus cap). Samsung began "warming down" equipment, prioritizing big customers like Nvidia
  • A Korean court granted an injunction to keep machines running — worst case avoided, but any short DRAM disruption could ripple across the AI trade and push memory prices higher
  • Likely beneficiary: US-based Micron (~23% of DRAM) could pick up customers Samsung can't serve
  • KOSPI: best-performing market in the world last year (+76%) and already +74% this year
💡 Big-picture link

This May blow-off is the opposite bookend of Korea's July 8 bear-market entry (−20% from the peak) — the starting point of the "hottest market → biggest correction" arc.

AI / Legal
(5/18) Musk v. OpenAI tossed by the jury in just two hours
Brew Markets / Morning Brew ▾
After a three-week trial, the jury needed only two hours to conclude that Elon Musk's suit against OpenAI should be thrown out — he waited too long to accuse Sam Altman of fraud, missing the three-year statute of limitations. The verdict is advisory, but the judge agreed and promised to dismiss "on the spot."
  • A blow to Musk just as SpaceX preps a summer IPO; a win for OpenAI ahead of a likely ~$1T IPO later this year
  • xAI still trails OpenAI by a wide margin

The week opened with oil and Treasury yields climbing together, reviving inflation jitters. The US 10-year sat around 4.6% (near a 1-year high) and Bitcoin was −12% YTD. Trump pressed Iran on a 20-year nuclear moratorium ("the clock is ticking").

  • JPMorgan: Americans have created $68T of new wealth since 2019, propping up the economy
Energy / Oil
(5/19) JPMorgan's "illusion of plenty" — 11 weeks into the Hormuz closure, a September oil-crisis warning
Brew Markets ▾
It's been 11 weeks since the Strait of Hormuz closed and the oil market has held up — but JPMorgan warns of an "illusion of plenty." Of the world's 8.4B barrels in storage, only ~10% is actually usable in an emergency; the rest is tied up in pipeline fill, tank bottoms, and strategic reserves.
  • Inventories ~35% depleted as of late April. On this path: "operational stress" in early June and the "operation floor" breached by September → pipelines lose pressure, refineries shutter, fuel rationing
  • JPMorgan sees Hormuz reopening by September — not from a peace deal but because it "has to." Even then, the new crude floor is far above pre-war levels at ~$80/barrel
  • Crude ~$104 on the day; 30-yr yields spiking (below) on inflation fears. Treasury Sec. Bessent urged the G7 to sanction Iran aggressively
💡 Big-picture link

The exact opposite of the "ceasefire → oil down → inflation down" pillar of the Q2 rally. The risk that materialized in July's ceasefire collapse and Brent spike was already flagged here in May.

Rates / Consumer
(5/19) US 30-yr yield hits 5.19%, a 19-year high · consumer sentiment at a record low · Home Depot softens
Brew Markets / Morning Brew ▾
Inflation-comeback fears hammered bonds: the 30-year Treasury yield briefly touched 5.19%, its highest in 19 years, and the S&P and Nasdaq fell for a third straight day. Consumer data also weakened — US consumer sentiment dropped to a record low earlier in the month.
  • Home Depot Q1: EPS $3.43 (slight beat) but comparable sales +0.6% (below +0.8% expected) with soft guidance; housing still in a rut on high mortgage rates and prices
  • Rising oil pushed grocery costs 3–4% higher (UBS). Watch Target (20th) and Walmart (Thu); value retailers seen benefiting

  • Seagate fell on comments that new factories "take too long," dragging memory/chip names lower
AI / Infra
(5/19) Google + Blackstone AI-cloud JV — taking on Nvidia and the neoclouds with TPUs
Brew Markets ▾
A $4.75T Google and the world's largest alternative asset manager, Blackstone, are teaming up to build a new AI cloud-infrastructure company. Google brings its TPUs (custom AI chips); Blackstone brings $5B to launch it, offering "compute-as-a-service" for anyone running AI models.
  • Direct hit on compute-renting "neoclouds" (CoreWeave −3.82% on the day) and on Nvidia GPUs. Google is commercializing TPUs as GPU alternatives — already selling them to Anthropic and Meta
  • Blackstone is the largest private datacenter owner and has been pushing hard into AI (including a recent Anthropic JV)
💡 Big-picture link

AI-infra money (power, datacenters, chips) is pooling into Big Tech + private capital — an early sign of the "AI capex financing rush" that runs into Alphabet's June share issuance and Nvidia's borrowing.

(5/20) Meta cuts ~10% of staff (~8,000) — mass layoffs "to fund AI"
Morning Brew ▾
Meta laid off about 10% of its workforce — roughly 8,000 of ~78,000 — while moving 7,000 employees to AI initiatives and closing 6,000 open roles. Unlike the 2022 "Covid over-hiring" cuts, these are explicitly meant to free up cash for AI: Meta has pledged up to $145B on AI this year.
  • Morale at rock bottom: some staff are begging for the 16-week severance; a decade-long employee said "I tend to cry in the shower"

  • Same day, stocks fell on inflation fears (Nasdaq −0.84%); even Nvidia slipped −0.77% ahead of earnings
💡 Big-picture link

The clearest symbol of the "cut jobs, pour it into AI" Big Tech playbook. The AI-capex-vs-headcount squeeze that runs into July was already stark in May.

Policy / Quantum
(5/22) Trump admin takes $2B in equity stakes across 9 quantum firms — shares jump up to 31%
Morning Brew ▾
The US government said it will invest $2B in nine quantum-computing companies (IBM, D-Wave, Rigetti, Infleqtion and others) in exchange for equity — funded via the CHIPS Act. Their shares jumped as much as 31%. It extends the administration's direct-stake push into "national-security-critical" industries, after last year's 10% Intel stake.
  • IBM gets $1B (matched, to build a quantum-chip maker in Albany, NY); GlobalFoundries $375M; D-Wave, Rigetti and Infleqtion $100M each
  • McKinsey sees quantum adding $2.7T to the global economy by 2035, though error rates keep it from commercial use — Huang says practical use is ~20 years off, Bill Gates says as little as 3
  • Week's close: indexes edged up on Iran-talk optimism; Spotify +13% on a Universal AI deal; Walmart gave weak guidance on high gas prices; Eli Lilly's retatrutide showed 28% weight loss
💡 Big-picture link

Government buying equity in chips, critical minerals and now quantum — the spread of "state capitalism." Fiscal intervention justified by AI and security has become a market theme in itself.

Bonds
(5/19–20) Authers: "The great bond car wreck — in slow motion" — 30-year yields at a 19-year high may end the cheap-money era
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Obscured by AI euphoria, mid-May's real event was in bonds. The 30-year Treasury yield burst through to a 19-year high, breaking long-held landmarks one by one. Authers called it "the great bond car wreck — in slow motion": not a crisis that detonates, but destruction that proceeds slowly and surely.
  • What it means: war-driven inflation plus fiscal strain could end the era of cheap funds. Rising long rates lift the discount rate on every asset
  • The irony of the moment: bonds were sounding an alarm while equities stayed serene on AI — two markets living in different worlds
💡 Big-picture link

It returns as Warsh's hawkish surprise lifts real yields on June 18, and as the cause of software's collapse on June 24 ("rates higher-for-longer than expected strain the debt"). The bass note running under the whole summer.
